**The Jewish Torah: Genesis Unit I (1:1-2:3)**

1:1 In the beginning, God created the heavens and the earth.  
1:2 The earth was formless and empty, with darkness over the surface of the deep, and the Spirit of God was hovering over the waters.  
1:3 God said, "Let there be light," and there was light.  
1:4 God saw that the light was good, and He separated the light from the darkness.  
1:5 God called the light "day," and the darkness He called "night." There was evening, and there was morning—the first day.

1:14 God said, "Let there be lights in the sky to separate the day from the night, and let them serve as signs to mark seasons, days, and years,  
1:15 and let them be lights in the sky to give light on the earth." And it was so.  
1:16 God made two great lights—the greater light to govern the day and the lesser light to govern the night. He also made the stars.  
1:17 God set them in the sky to give light on the earth,  
1:18 to govern the day and the night, and to separate light from darkness. God saw that it was good.  
1:19 There was evening, and there was morning—the fourth day.

1:6 God said, "Let there be a vault between the waters to separate water from water."  
1:7 So God made the vault and separated the water under the vault from the water above it. And it was so.  
1:8 God called the vault "sky." There was evening, and there was morning—the second day.

1:20 God said, "Let the water teem with living creatures, and let birds fly above the earth across the vault of the sky."  
1:21 So God created the great creatures of the sea and every living thing with which the water teems and that moves about in it, according to their kinds, and every winged bird according to its kind. And God saw that it was good.  
1:22 God blessed them and said, "Be fruitful and increase in number and fill the water in the seas, and let the birds increase on the earth."  
1:23 There was evening, and there was morning—the fifth day.

1:9 God said, "Let the water under the sky be gathered to one place, and let dry ground appear." And it was so.  
1:10 God called the dry ground "land," and the gathered waters He called "seas." And God saw that it was good.

1:24 God said, "Let the land produce living creatures according to their kinds: the livestock, the creatures that move along the ground, and the wild animals, each according to its kind." And it was so.  
1:25 God made the wild animals according to their kinds, the livestock according to their kinds, and all the creatures that move along the ground according to their kinds. And God saw that it was good.

1:11 God said, "Let the land produce vegetation: seed-bearing plants and trees on the land that bear fruit with seed in it, according to their kinds." And it was so.  
1:12 The land produced vegetation: plants bearing seed according to their kinds and trees bearing fruit with seed in it according to their kinds. And God saw that it was good.  
1:13 There was evening, and there was morning—the third day.

1:26 God said, "Let us make mankind in our image, in our likeness, so that they may rule over the fish in the sea and the birds in the sky, over the livestock and all the wild animals, and over all the creatures that move along the ground."  
1:27 So God created mankind in His own image, in the image of God He created them; male and female He created them.  
1:28 God blessed them and said to them, "Be fruitful and increase in number; fill the earth and subdue it. Rule over the fish in the sea and the birds in the sky and over every living creature that moves on the ground."  
1:29 God said, "I give you every seed-bearing plant on the face of the whole earth and every tree that has fruit with seed in it. They will be yours for food.  
1:30 And to all the beasts of the earth and all the birds in the sky and all the creatures that move along the ground—everything that has the breath of life in it—I give every green plant for food." And it was so.  
1:31 God saw all that He had made, and it was very good. There was evening, and there was morning—the sixth day.

2:1 Thus the heavens and the earth were completed in all their vast array.  
2:2 By the seventh day, God had finished the work He had been doing; so on the seventh day, He rested from all His work.  
2:3 Then God blessed the seventh day and made it holy, because on it He rested from all the work of creating that He had done.

**Genesis Unit II (2:4-4:26)**

2:4 This is the account of the heavens and the earth when they were created, when the LORD God made the earth and the heavens.  
2:5 No shrub had yet appeared on the earth and no plant had yet sprung up, for the LORD God had not sent rain on the earth and there was no one to work the ground,  
2:6 but streams came up from the earth and watered the whole surface of the ground.  
2:7 Then the LORD God formed a man from the dust of the ground and breathed into his nostrils the breath of life, and the man became a living being.

2:18 The LORD God said, "It is not good for the man to be alone. I will make a helper suitable for him."  
2:19 Now the LORD God had formed out of the ground all the wild animals and all the birds in the sky. He brought them to the man to see what he would name them; and whatever the man called each living creature, that was its name.  
2:20 So the man gave names to all the livestock, the birds in the sky, and all the wild animals. But for Adam, no suitable helper was found.

2:8 Now the LORD God had planted a garden in the east, in Eden; and there He put the man He had formed.  
2:9 The LORD God made all kinds of trees grow out of the ground—trees that were pleasing to the eye and good for food. In the middle of the garden were the tree of life and the tree of the knowledge of good and evil.  
2:10 A river watering the garden flowed from Eden; from there it was separated into four headwaters.  
2:11 The name of the first is the Pishon; it winds through the entire land of Havilah, where there is gold.  
2:12 The gold of that land is good; aromatic resin and onyx are also there.  
2:13 The name of the second river is the Gihon; it winds through the entire land of Cush.  
2:14 The name of the third river is the Tigris; it runs along the east side of Ashur. And the fourth river is the Euphrates.  
2:15 The LORD God took the man and put him in the Garden of Eden to work it and take care of it.  
2:16 And the LORD God commanded the man, "You are free to eat from any tree in the garden;  
2:17 but you must not eat from the tree of the knowledge of good and evil, for when you eat from it you will certainly die."

2:21 So the LORD God caused the man to fall into a deep sleep; and while he was sleeping, He took one of the man's ribs and then closed up the place with flesh.  
2:22 Then the LORD God made a woman from the rib He had taken out of the man, and He brought her to the man.  
2:23 The man said, "This is now bone of my bones and flesh of my flesh; she shall be called 'woman,' for she was taken out of man."  
2:24 That is why a man leaves his father and mother and is united to his wife, and they become one flesh.  
2:25 Adam and his wife were both naked, and they felt no shame.

3:1 Now the serpent was more crafty than any of the wild animals the LORD God had made. He said to the woman, "Did God really say, 'You must not eat from any tree in the garden'?"  
3:2 The woman said to the serpent, "We may eat fruit from the trees in the garden,  
3:3 but God did say, 'You must not eat fruit from the tree that is in the middle of the garden, and you must not touch it, or you will die.'"  
3:4 "You will not certainly die," the serpent said to the woman.  
3:5 "For God knows that when you eat from it your eyes will be opened, and you will be like God, knowing good and evil."  
3:6 When the woman saw that the fruit of the tree was good for food and pleasing to the eye, and also desirable for gaining wisdom, she took some and ate it. She also gave some to her husband, who was with her, and he ate it.  
3:7 Then the eyes of both of them were opened, and they realized they were naked; so they sewed fig leaves together and made coverings for themselves.

4:1 Adam made love to his wife Eve, and she became pregnant and gave birth to Cain. She said, "With the help of the LORD I have brought forth a man."  
4:2 Later she gave birth to his brother Abel. Now Abel kept flocks, and Cain worked the soil.  
4:3 In the course of time, Cain brought some of the fruits of the soil as an offering to the LORD.  
4:4 And Abel also brought an offering—fat portions from some of the firstborn of his flock. The LORD looked with favor on Abel and his offering,  
4:5 but on Cain and his offering He did not look with favor. So Cain was very angry, and his face was downcast.  
4:6 Then the LORD said to Cain, "Why are you angry? Why is your face downcast?  
4:7 If you do what is right, will you not be accepted? But if you do not do what is right, sin is crouching at your door; it desires to have you, but you must rule over it."  
4:8 Now Cain said to his brother Abel, "Let's go out to the field." While they were in the field, Cain attacked his brother Abel and killed him.

3:8 Then the man and his wife heard the sound of the LORD God as He was walking in the garden in the cool of the day, and they hid from the LORD God among the trees of the garden.**Genesis Unit II (2:4-4:26)**

2:4 This is the story of the creation of the heavens and the earth when the LORD God made them.  
2:5 No plants or shrubs had yet grown on the earth because the LORD God had not sent rain, and there was no one to cultivate the land.  
2:6 But streams came up from the earth and watered the entire surface of the ground.  
2:7 Then the LORD God formed a man from the dust of the ground and breathed life into his nostrils, making him a living being.

2:18 The LORD God said, "It is not good for the man to be alone. I will make a suitable helper for him."  
2:19 The LORD God had formed all the wild animals and birds from the ground. He brought them to the man to see what he would name them, and whatever the man called each living creature, that was its name.  
2:20 The man named all the livestock, birds, and wild animals, but no suitable helper was found for Adam.

2:8 The LORD God planted a garden in Eden, in the east, and placed the man He had formed there.  
2:9 The LORD God made all kinds of trees grow out of the ground—trees that were pleasing to the eye and good for food. In the middle of the garden were the tree of life and the tree of the knowledge of good and evil.  
2:10 A river flowed from Eden to water the garden, and from there it divided into four headwaters.  
2:11 The first river is the Pishon, which winds through the entire land of Havilah, where there is gold.  
2:12 The gold of that land is good; aromatic resin and onyx are also there.  
2:13 The second river is the Gihon, which winds through the entire land of Cush.  
2:14 The third river is the Tigris, which runs along the east side of Ashur. The fourth river is the Euphrates.  
2:15 The LORD God took the man and put him in the Garden of Eden to work it and take care of it.  
2:16 And the LORD God commanded the man, "You are free to eat from any tree in the garden;  
2:17 but you must not eat from the tree of the knowledge of good and evil, for when you eat from it you will certainly die."

2:21 So the LORD God caused the man to fall into a deep sleep; and while he was sleeping, He took one of the man's ribs and then closed up the place with flesh.  
2:22 Then the LORD God made a woman from the rib He had taken out of the man, and He brought her to the man.  
2:23 The man said, "This is now bone of my bones and flesh of my flesh; she shall be called 'woman,' for she was taken out of man."  
2:24 That is why a man leaves his father and mother and is united to his wife, and they become one flesh.  
2:25 Adam and his wife were both naked, and they felt no shame.

3:1 Now the serpent was more crafty than any of the wild animals the LORD God had made. He said to the woman, "Did God really say, 'You must not eat from any tree in the garden'?"  
3:2 The woman said to the serpent, "We may eat fruit from the trees in the garden,  
3:3 but God did say, 'You must not eat fruit from the tree that is in the middle of the garden, and you must not touch it, or you will die.'"  
3:4 "You will not certainly die," the serpent said to the woman.  
3:5 "For God knows that when you eat from it your eyes will be opened, and you will be like God, knowing good and evil."  
3:6 When the woman saw that the fruit of the tree was good for food and pleasing to the eye, and also desirable for gaining wisdom, she took some and ate it. She also gave some to her husband, who was with her, and he ate it.  
3:7 Then the eyes of both of them were opened, and they realized they were naked; so they sewed fig leaves together and made coverings for themselves.

4:1 Adam made love to his wife Eve, and she became pregnant and gave birth to Cain. She said, "With the help of the LORD I have brought forth a man."  
4:2 Later she gave birth to his brother Abel. Now Abel kept flocks, and Cain worked the soil.  
4:3 In the course of time, Cain brought some of the fruits of the soil as an offering to the LORD.  
4:4 And Abel also brought an offering—fat portions from some of the firstborn of his flock. The LORD looked with favor on Abel and his offering,  
4:5 but on Cain and his offering He did not look with favor. So Cain was very angry, and his face was downcast.  
4:6 Then the LORD said to Cain, "Why are you angry? Why is your face downcast?  
4:7 If you do what is right, will you not be accepted? But if you do not do what is right, sin is crouching at your door; it desires to have you, but you must rule over it."  
4:8 Now Cain said to his brother Abel, "Let's go out to the field." While they were in the field, Cain attacked his brother Abel and killed him.

3:8 Then the man and his wife heard the sound of the LORD God as He was walking in the garden in the cool of the day, and they hid from the LORD God among the trees of the garden.  
3:9 But the LORD God called to the man, "Where are you?"  
3:10 He answered, "I heard you in the garden, and I was afraid because I was naked; so I hid."  
3:11 And He said, "Who told you that you were naked? Have you eaten from the tree that I commanded you not to eat from?"  
3:12 The man said, "The woman you put here with me—she gave me some fruit from the tree, and I ate it."  
3:13 Then the LORD God said to the woman, "What is this you have done?"  
The woman said, "The serpent deceived me, and I ate."  
3:14 So the LORD God said to the serpent, "Because you have done this, cursed are you above all livestock and all wild animals! You will crawl on your belly and you will eat dust all the days of your life.  
3:15 And I will put enmity between you and the woman, and between your offspring and hers; he will crush your head, and you will strike his heel."  
3:16 To the woman He said, "I will make your pains in childbearing very severe; with painful labor you will give birth to children. Your desire will be for your husband, and he will rule over you."  
3:17 To Adam He said, "Because you listened to your wife and ate fruit from the tree about which I commanded you, 'You must not eat from it,' cursed is the ground because of you; through painful toil you will eat food from it all the days of your life.  
3:18 It will produce thorns and thistles for you, and you will eat the plants of the field.  
3:19 By the sweat of your brow you will eat your food until you return to the ground, since from it you were taken; for dust you are and to dust you will return."  
3:20 Adam named his wife Eve, because she would become the mother of all the living.  
3:21 The LORD God made garments of skin for Adam and his wife and clothed them.  
3:22 And the LORD God said, "The man has now become like one of us, knowing good and evil. He must not be allowed to reach out his hand and take also from the tree of life and eat, and live forever."  
3:23 So the LORD God banished him from the Garden of Eden to work the ground from which he had been taken.  
3:24 After He drove the man out, He placed on the east side of the Garden of Eden cherubim and a flaming sword flashing back and forth to guard the way to the tree of life.

4:9 Then the LORD said to Cain, "Where is your brother Abel?"  
"I don't know," he replied. "Am I my brother's keeper?"  
4:10 The LORD said, "What have you done? Listen! Your brother's blood cries out to me from the ground.  
4:11 Now you are under a curse and driven from the ground, which opened its mouth to receive your brother's blood from your hand.  
4:12 When you work the ground, it will no longer yield its crops for you. You will be a restless wanderer on the earth."  
4:13 Cain said to the LORD, "My punishment is more than I can bear.  
4:14 Today you are driving me from the land, and I will be hidden from your presence; I will be a restless wanderer on the earth, and whoever finds me will kill me."  
4:15 But the LORD said to him, "Not so; anyone who kills Cain will suffer vengeance seven times over." Then the LORD put a mark on Cain so that no one who found him would kill him.  
4:16 So Cain went out from the LORD's presence and lived in the land of Nod, east of Eden.  
4:17 Cain made love to his wife, and she became pregnant and gave birth to Enoch. Cain was then building a city, and he named it after his son Enoch.  
4:18 To Enoch was born Irad, and Irad was the father of Mehujael, and Mehujael was the father of Methushael, and Methushael was the father of Lamech.  
4:19 Lamech married two women, one named Adah and the other Zillah.  
4:20 Adah gave birth to Jabal; he was the father of those who live in tents and raise livestock.  
4:21 His brother's name was Jubal; he was the father of all who play stringed instruments and pipes.  
4:22 Zillah also had a son, Tubal-Cain, who forged all kinds of tools out of bronze and iron. Tubal-Cain's sister was Naamah.  
4:23 Lamech said to his wives, "Adah and Zillah, listen to me; wives of Lamech, hear my words. I have killed a man for wounding me, a young man for injuring me.  
4:24 If Cain is avenged seven times, then Lamech seventy-seven times."  
4:25 Adam made love to his wife again, and she gave birth to a son and named him Seth, saying, "God has granted me another child in place of Abel, since Cain killed him."  
4:26 Seth also had a son, and he named him Enosh. At that time people began to call on the name of the LORD.God said to Noah, "I will establish my covenant with you. You, your sons, your wife, and your sons' wives will enter the ark. Bring two of every living creature, male and female, to keep them alive with you. Gather food for you and them." Noah did everything God commanded.

God told Noah, "Enter the ark with your family, for I have seen you as righteous in this generation. Take seven pairs of every clean animal and one pair of every unclean animal, and seven pairs of every bird, to keep their species alive. In seven days, I will send rain for forty days and nights, and I will wipe out every living thing I have made." Noah did as God commanded.

Noah was 600 years old when the floodwaters came. On the 17th day of the second month, the springs of the great deep burst forth, and the windows of heaven opened. Rain fell for forty days and nights. Noah, his family, and all the animals entered the ark, and God shut them in.

The floodwaters increased, lifting the ark high above the earth. The waters rose and covered the mountains by more than twenty feet. Every living thing on the earth perished—birds, livestock, wild animals, and all mankind. Only Noah and those with him in the ark survived.

After forty days, Noah opened a window and sent out a raven, which kept flying back and forth until the waters dried up. He also sent out a dove, but it found no place to rest and returned to the ark. After seven days, he sent the dove again, and it returned with an olive leaf, showing the waters had receded. He waited another seven days and sent the dove out again, and it did not return.

In the 601st year of Noah's life, on the first day of the first month, the waters had dried up. By the 27th day of the second month, the earth was completely dry. God told Noah to come out of the ark with his family and all the animals so they could multiply on the earth.

Noah built an altar and offered sacrifices to God. God was pleased and promised never to curse the ground again or destroy all living creatures. He established a covenant with Noah and his descendants, symbolized by a rainbow, promising never to flood the earth again.

Noah's sons, Shem, Ham, and Japheth, repopulated the earth. Noah planted a vineyard, drank wine, and became drunk, lying uncovered in his tent. Ham saw his father's nakedness and told his brothers, who covered Noah without looking. When Noah awoke, he cursed Ham's son Canaan and blessed Shem and Japheth.Noah's sons, Shem, Ham, and Japheth, covered their father's nakedness without looking. When Noah woke up and realized what his youngest son had done, he cursed Ham's son, Canaan, to be a servant to his brothers. He blessed the Lord, the God of Shem, and prayed for Japheth's prosperity. Noah lived 350 years after the flood, making his total lifespan 950 years before he died.

The descendants of Noah's sons spread out after the flood. Japheth's sons included Gomer, Magog, Madai, Javan, Tubal, Meshech, and Tiras. Gomer's sons were Ashkenaz, Riphath, and Togarmah. Javan's sons were Elishah, Tarshish, Kittim, and Dodanim. These families spread out into their lands, each with its own language and nation.

Ham's sons were Cush, Mizraim, Put, and Canaan. Cush's sons included Seba, Havilah, Sabtah, Raamah, and Sabteca. Raamah's sons were Sheba and Dedan. Cush fathered Nimrod, who became a mighty hunter and established his kingdom in Babel, Erech, Accad, and Calneh in Shinar. From there, Asshur built Nineveh, Rehoboth-Ir, Calah, and Resen, which became a great city. Mizraim's descendants included Ludim, Anamim, Lehabim, Naphtuhim, Pathrusim, Casluhim (from whom the Philistines came), and Caphtorim. Canaan's descendants included Zidon, Heth, and various tribes like the Jebusites, Amorites, and Girgashites. The Canaanite territory extended from Zidon to Gaza and towards Sodom, Gomorrah, Admah, and Zeboiim.

Shem's descendants included Elam, Asshur, Arpachshad, Lud, and Aram. Aram's sons were Uz, Hul, Gether, and Mash. Arpachshad fathered Shelah, who fathered Eber. Eber had two sons, Peleg (named because the earth was divided in his time) and Joktan. Joktan's sons included Almodad, Sheleph, Hazarmaveth, Jerah, Hadoram, Uzal, Diklah, Obal, Abimael, Sheba, Ophir, Havilah, and Jobab. They lived from Mesha to Sephar in the eastern mountains. These families spread out into their lands, each with its own language and nation.

The whole earth initially spoke one language. As people moved east, they settled in Shinar and decided to build a city and a tower reaching the heavens to make a name for themselves and avoid being scattered. God saw their city and tower and decided to confuse their language, causing them to stop building and scatter across the earth. The city was called Babel because there God confused the language of the whole world.

Shem's lineage continued with Arpachshad, who fathered Shelah, who fathered Eber, who fathered Peleg, who fathered Reu, who fathered Serug, who fathered Nahor, who fathered Terah. Terah fathered Abram, Nahor, and Haran. Haran fathered Lot but died in Ur of the Chaldees. Abram married Sarai, and Nahor married Milcah. Sarai was barren. Terah took Abram, Lot, and Sarai from Ur to Canaan but settled in Haran, where he died at 205 years old.

God told Abram to leave his country and family for a land He would show him, promising to make Abram a great nation and bless him. Abram obeyed, taking Sarai, Lot, and their possessions to Canaan. God appeared to Abram in Shechem, promising the land to his descendants. Abram built an altar there.

A famine forced Abram to go to Egypt. Fearing for his life because of Sarai's beauty, he asked her to say she was his sister. Pharaoh took Sarai into his house, treating Abram well because of her. But God plagued Pharaoh's house, leading Pharaoh to return Sarai to Abram and send them away with their possessions. Abram returned to the Negev, rich in livestock, silver, and gold.Abram moved his camp to the mountain east of Bethel, with Bethel to the west and Ai to the east. He built an altar there and called on the name of the Lord. Abram continued his journey southward.

Abram returned to the place between Bethel and Ai where he had first set up his tent and built an altar. He called on the name of the Lord again.

Lot, who traveled with Abram, also had flocks, herds, and tents. The land couldn't support both of them living together because they had too many possessions. This led to conflicts between Abram's and Lot's herdsmen. The Canaanites and Perizzites were also living in the land at that time.

Abram suggested to Lot that they separate to avoid conflict, offering Lot the choice of land. Lot chose the well-watered plain of the Jordan, which was like the garden of the Lord and the land of Egypt. Lot moved eastward, and they parted ways.

After Lot left, the Lord told Abram to look around from where he was standing. God promised to give all the land he could see to Abram and his descendants forever. God also promised to make Abram's descendants as numerous as the dust of the earth. Abram was told to walk through the land, as it would all be given to him.

Abram settled in Canaan, while Lot lived in the cities of the plain, pitching his tents near Sodom. The men of Sodom were wicked and sinned greatly against the Lord.

Abram moved his camp to the terebinths of Mamre in Hebron and built an altar to the Lord there.

During the time of Amraphel king of Shinar, Arioch king of Ellasar, Chedorlaomer king of Elam, and Tidal king of Goiim, these kings waged war against Bera king of Sodom, Birsha king of Gomorrah, Shinab king of Admah, Shemeber king of Zeboiim, and the king of Bela (Zoar). They joined forces in the Valley of Siddim (the Salt Sea). They served Chedorlaomer for twelve years but rebelled in the thirteenth year. In the fourteenth year, Chedorlaomer and his allies defeated the Rephaim, Zuzim, Emim, and Horites, and then turned back to Kadesh and defeated the Amalekites and Amorites.

The kings of Sodom, Gomorrah, Admah, Zeboiim, and Bela set up battle lines in the Valley of Siddim against Chedorlaomer and his allies. The Valley of Siddim was full of tar pits, and the kings of Sodom and Gomorrah fled, falling into them, while the rest escaped to the mountains.

One who escaped reported to Abram that Lot had been captured. Abram gathered 318 trained men from his household and pursued the captors as far as Dan. He divided his forces at night, attacked, and pursued them to Hobah, north of Damascus. Abram recovered all the goods and brought back Lot, his possessions, the women, and the other people.

The king of Sodom went out to meet Abram in the Valley of Shaveh (the King's Valley) after Abram's victory. Melchizedek, king of Salem and priest of God Most High, brought bread and wine and blessed Abram, praising God for delivering Abram's enemies into his hand. Abram gave Melchizedek a tenth of everything.

The king of Sodom offered Abram the goods but asked for the people. Abram refused to take anything, not even a thread or a sandal strap, to avoid the king of Sodom claiming he made Abram rich. Abram only accepted what his men had eaten and the share for his allies, Aner, Eshcol, and Mamre.

After these events, the word of the Lord came to Abram in a vision, telling him not to fear, as God was his shield and his reward would be great. Abram expressed concern about being childless and having his servant Eliezer of Damascus as his heir. God assured Abram that his own offspring would be his heir and promised that his descendants would be as numerous as the stars. Abram believed the Lord, and it was credited to him as righteousness.

God reminded Abram that He brought him out of Ur of the Chaldees to give him this land to inherit. Abram asked for a sign to confirm this promise. God instructed him to bring a heifer, a goat, a ram, a turtledove, and a young pigeon. Abram brought these animals, cut them in half, and arranged the halves opposite each other, but did not cut the birds. Birds of prey came down on the carcasses, but Abram drove them away.

As the sun was setting, Abram fell into a deep sleep, and a great darkness came over him. God told Abram that his descendants would be strangers in a foreign land, enslaved and oppressed for 400 years. However, God would judge the nation they serve, and afterward, they would come out with great possessions. Abram would die in peace at a good old age, and in the fourth generation, his descendants would return to this land, as the sin of the Amorites had not yet reached its full measure.

When the sun set and darkness fell, a smoking firepot and a blazing torch appeared and passed between the pieces of the animals. On that day, the Lord made a covenant with Abram, promising to give his descendants the land from the river of Egypt to the great river, the Euphrates, including the land of the Kenites, Kenizzites, Kadmonites, Hittites, Perizzites, Rephaim, Amorites, Canaanites, Girgashites, and Jebusites.

Sarai, Abram's wife, had borne him no children. She had an Egyptian maidservant named Hagar. Sarai suggested that Abram sleep with Hagar to build a family through her. Abram agreed, and Hagar conceived. When Hagar realized she was pregnant, she began to despise Sarai. Sarai blamed Abram for the situation, and Abram told her to do whatever she thought best with Hagar. Sarai mistreated Hagar, causing her to flee.

The angel of the Lord found Hagar near a spring in the desert, on the way to Shur. The angel asked Hagar where she had come from and where she was going. Hagar replied that she was running away from her mistress, Sarai.The angel of the Lord told Hagar to return to her mistress and submit to her. The angel also promised to greatly increase her descendants so they would be too numerous to count. The angel said she was pregnant and would have a son named Ishmael because the Lord had heard her misery. Ishmael would be a wild man, in conflict with everyone, but living near his relatives.

Hagar called the Lord who spoke to her "You are the God who sees me," because she said, "I have now seen the One who sees me." The well where this happened was called Beer-lahai-roi, located between Kadesh and Bered. Hagar bore Abram a son, and Abram named him Ishmael. Abram was 86 years old when Ishmael was born.

When Abram was 99 years old, the Lord appeared to him and said, "I am God Almighty; walk before me faithfully and be blameless. I will make my covenant between me and you and will greatly increase your numbers." Abram fell facedown, and God said to him, "As for me, this is my covenant with you: You will be the father of many nations. No longer will you be called Abram; your name will be Abraham, for I have made you a father of many nations. I will make you very fruitful; I will make nations of you, and kings will come from you. I will establish my covenant as an everlasting covenant between me and you and your descendants after you for generations to come, to be your God and the God of your descendants after you. The whole land of Canaan, where you now reside as a foreigner, I will give as an everlasting possession to you and your descendants after you; and I will be their God."

God also said to Abraham, "As for Sarai your wife, you are no longer to call her Sarai; her name will be Sarah. I will bless her and will surely give you a son by her. I will bless her so that she will be the mother of nations; kings of peoples will come from her." Abraham fell facedown; he laughed and said to himself, "Will a son be born to a man a hundred years old? Will Sarah bear a child at the age of ninety?" And Abraham said to God, "If only Ishmael might live under your blessing!" Then God said, "Yes, but your wife Sarah will bear you a son, and you will call him Isaac. I will establish my covenant with him as an everlasting covenant for his descendants after him. And as for Ishmael, I have heard you: I will surely bless him; I will make him fruitful and will greatly increase his numbers. He will be the father of twelve rulers, and I will make him into a great nation. But my covenant I will establish with Isaac, whom Sarah will bear to you by this time next year." When he had finished speaking with Abraham, God went up from him.

Then God said to Abraham, "As for you, you must keep my covenant, you and your descendants after you for the generations to come. This is my covenant with you and your descendants after you, the covenant you are to keep: Every male among you shall be circumcised. You are to undergo circumcision, and it will be the sign of the covenant between me and you. For the generations to come, every male among you who is eight days old must be circumcised, including those born in your household or bought with money from a foreigner—those who are not your offspring. Whether born in your household or bought with your money, they must be circumcised. My covenant in your flesh is to be an everlasting covenant. Any uncircumcised male, who has not been circumcised in the flesh, will be cut off from his people; he has broken my covenant."

On that very day, Abraham took his son Ishmael and all those born in his household or bought with his money, every male in his household, and circumcised them, as God told him. Abraham was 99 years old when he was circumcised, and his son Ishmael was 13. Abraham and his son Ishmael were both circumcised on that same day. And every male in Abraham's household, including those born in his household or bought from a foreigner, was circumcised with him.

The Lord appeared to Abraham near the great trees of Mamre while he was sitting at the entrance to his tent in the heat of the day. Abraham looked up and saw three men standing nearby. When he saw them, he hurried from the entrance of his tent to meet them and bowed low to the ground. He said, "If I have found favor in your eyes, my lord, do not pass your servant by. Let a little water be brought, and then you may all wash your feet and rest under this tree. Let me get you something to eat, so you can be refreshed and then go on your way—now that you have come to your servant." "Very well," they answered, "do as you say."

So Abraham hurried into the tent to Sarah. "Quick," he said, "get three seahs of the finest flour and knead it and bake some bread." Then he ran to the herd and selected a choice, tender calf and gave it to a servant, who hurried to prepare it. He then brought some curds and milk and the calf that had been prepared, and set these before them. While they ate, he stood near them under a tree.

When the men got up to leave, they looked down toward Sodom, and Abraham walked along with them to see them on their way. Then the Lord said, "Shall I hide from Abraham what I am about to do? Abraham will surely become a great and powerful nation, and all nations on earth will be blessed through him. For I have chosen him, so that he will direct his children and his household after him to keep the way of the Lord by doing what is right and just, so that the Lord will bring about for Abraham what he has promised him."

Then the Lord said, "The outcry against Sodom and Gomorrah is so great and their sin so grievous that I will go down and see if what they have done is as bad as the outcry that has reached me. If not, I will know." The men turned away and went toward Sodom, but Abraham remained standing before the Lord. Then Abraham approached him and said: "Will you sweep away the righteous with the wicked? What if there are fifty righteous people in the city? Will you really sweep it away and not spare the place for the sake of the fifty righteous people in it? Far be it from you to do such a thing—to kill the righteous with the wicked, treating the righteous and the wicked alike. Far be it from you! Will not the Judge of all the earth do right?"

The Lord said, "If I find fifty righteous people in the city of Sodom, I will spare the whole place for their sake." Then Abraham spoke up again: "Now that I have been so bold as to speak to the Lord, though I am nothing but dust and ashes, what if the number of the righteous is five less than fifty? Will you destroy the whole city for lack of five people?" "If I find forty-five there," he said, "I will not destroy it."

Once again he spoke to him, "What if only forty are found there?" He said, "For the sake of forty, I will not do it." Then he said, "May the Lord not be angry, but let me speak. What if only thirty can be found there?" He answered, "I will not do it if I find thirty there." Abraham said, "Now that I have been so bold as to speak to the Lord, what if only twenty can be found there?" He said, "For the sake of twenty, I will not destroy it." Then he said, "May the Lord not be angry, but let me speak just once more. What if only ten can be found there?" He answered, "For the sake of ten, I will not destroy it." When the Lord had finished speaking with Abraham, he left, and Abraham returned home.

Two angels arrived at Sodom in the evening, and Lot was sitting in the gateway of the city. When he saw them, he got up to meet them and bowed down with his face to the ground. "My lords," he said, "please turn aside to your servant's house. You can wash your feet and spend the night and then go on your way early in the morning."Lot insisted that the visitors stay at his house, and they finally agreed. He prepared a feast with unleavened bread, and they ate. The next morning, Abraham returned to the place where he had spoken with the Lord and saw smoke rising from Sodom and Gomorrah, like smoke from a furnace. God remembered Abraham and saved Lot from the destruction of the cities.

Before the visitors went to bed, the men of Sodom surrounded Lot's house, demanding to see the visitors. Lot went outside, closed the door behind him, and pleaded with them not to act wickedly. He even offered his two daughters to them instead, but they refused and tried to break down the door. The visitors pulled Lot back inside and struck the men outside with blindness, so they couldn't find the door.

The visitors told Lot to gather his family and leave the city because it was going to be destroyed. Lot warned his sons-in-law, but they thought he was joking. At dawn, the visitors urged Lot to hurry, and they led him, his wife, and his two daughters out of the city. They told them to flee to the mountains and not look back. Lot asked to escape to a nearby small city instead, and they agreed. The city was called Zoar.

As Lot reached Zoar, the Lord rained down burning sulfur on Sodom and Gomorrah, destroying the cities and everything in them. Lot's wife looked back and turned into a pillar of salt. Lot and his daughters left Zoar and lived in a cave in the mountains. The daughters, believing there were no men left to continue their family line, got their father drunk and slept with him. Both became pregnant. The older daughter had a son named Moab, the ancestor of the Moabites, and the younger daughter had a son named Ben-Ammi, the ancestor of the Ammonites.

Abraham moved to the Negev and lived between Kadesh and Shur, staying in Gerar. He told people that Sarah was his sister, so King Abimelech took her. God warned Abimelech in a dream that Sarah was married, and Abimelech returned her to Abraham, giving him sheep, cattle, and servants. Abimelech also gave Abraham land to live on and a thousand pieces of silver to Sarah as a sign of her innocence. Abraham prayed, and God healed Abimelech's household.

The Lord kept His promise to Sarah, and she bore a son, Isaac, to Abraham in his old age. Abraham circumcised Isaac when he was eight days old. Sarah said that God had brought her laughter, and everyone who heard would laugh with her. Isaac grew, and Abraham held a great feast when he was weaned. Sarah saw Hagar's son mocking and told Abraham to send Hagar and her son away, as he would not share in Isaac's inheritance. God told Abraham to listen to Sarah, promising to make a nation of Hagar's son as well. Abraham gave Hagar and her son provisions and sent them away.Hagar wandered in the wilderness of Beersheba after being sent away. When their water ran out, she placed her child under a shrub and sat a distance away, not wanting to watch him die. She wept, but God heard the boy's cries. An angel called to Hagar from heaven, reassuring her that God had heard the boy and promising to make him a great nation. God then opened her eyes to a well of water, and she filled her bottle and gave the boy a drink. The boy grew up in the wilderness, became an archer, and lived in the wilderness of Paran. His mother found him a wife from Egypt.

At that time, Abimelech and Phicol, his army commander, approached Abraham, acknowledging that God was with him in everything he did. They asked Abraham to swear not to deal falsely with them or their descendants, but to show the same kindness they had shown him. Abraham agreed.

Later, God tested Abraham by asking him to sacrifice his beloved son Isaac in the land of Moriah. Abraham obeyed, taking Isaac and two servants to the designated place. On the third day, Abraham saw the place from afar and told his servants to stay while he and Isaac went to worship. Isaac carried the wood for the offering, and Abraham carried the fire and knife. Isaac asked where the lamb for the offering was, and Abraham replied that God would provide it.

When they reached the place, Abraham built an altar, arranged the wood, and bound Isaac on it. As he was about to slay his son, an angel called out to stop him, acknowledging Abraham's fear of God. Abraham saw a ram caught in a thicket and offered it instead of Isaac. He named the place "The Lord Will Provide."

Abraham and Abimelech made a covenant at Beersheba after resolving a dispute over a well. Abraham planted a tamarisk tree there and called on the name of the Lord. He lived in the land of the Philistines for many days.

After these events, Abraham was informed that his brother Nahor's wife Milcah had borne children, including Bethuel, who fathered Rebekah. Sarah lived 127 years and died in Hebron. Abraham mourned her and negotiated with the Hittites to buy the cave of Machpelah from Ephron the Hittite for 400 shekels of silver as a burial site. Abraham buried Sarah there, securing the field and cave as his possession.

Abraham, now old and blessed by the Lord, instructed his servant to find a wife for Isaac from his homeland, not from the Canaanites. The servant questioned what to do if the woman was unwilling to come, and Abraham warned him not to take Isaac back to their former land. The Lord, who had guided Abraham, would send an angel to ensure the servant's success.Abraham's servant continued his journey to find a wife for Isaac. He prayed to God for guidance, asking that the woman who offers water to him and his camels be the chosen one. Before he finished praying, Rebekah appeared with a pitcher on her shoulder. She was beautiful and a virgin. The servant asked her for a drink, and she offered water to him and his camels, fulfilling his prayer.

The servant gave Rebekah a gold ring and bracelets and asked about her family. She revealed she was the daughter of Bethuel, Nahor's son. The servant bowed and thanked God for leading him to Abraham's relatives. Rebekah ran to tell her family, and her brother Laban welcomed the servant into their home.

The servant explained his mission and how God had guided him to Rebekah. Laban and Bethuel agreed that this was God's will and allowed Rebekah to go with the servant to marry Isaac. The servant gave gifts to Rebekah and her family, and they celebrated. The next morning, Rebekah agreed to leave immediately.

Rebekah and her maids traveled with the servant to meet Isaac. When they arrived, Isaac was meditating in a field. Rebekah saw him, covered herself with a veil, and met him. The servant recounted all that had happened. Isaac took Rebekah into his mother Sarah's tent, and she became his wife. He loved her, and she comforted him after his mother's death.

Abraham took another wife, Keturah, who bore him several children. He gave all his possessions to Isaac but provided gifts to his other sons and sent them away. Abraham lived 175 years and died at a good old age. Isaac and Ishmael buried him in the cave of Machpelah, which Abraham had purchased.After Abraham's death, God blessed his son Isaac, who lived near Beer-lahai-roi. 

**The Generations of Ishmael:**
Ishmael, Abraham's son with Hagar the Egyptian, had twelve sons: Nebaioth, Kedar, Adbeel, Mibsam, Mishma, Dumah, Massa, Hadad, Tema, Jetur, Naphish, and Kedem. These sons became leaders of their own tribes. Ishmael lived 137 years and passed away, and his descendants settled from Havilah to Shur, near Egypt.

**The Generations of Isaac:**
Isaac, Abraham's son, married Rebekah, daughter of Bethuel the Aramean, when he was forty. Rebekah was barren, but Isaac prayed, and she conceived twins. The twins struggled within her, and God told her that two nations were in her womb, with the older serving the younger. Esau, the firstborn, was red and hairy, while Jacob, the second, was born holding Esau's heel. Isaac was sixty when they were born.

Esau became a skilled hunter, while Jacob was a quiet man who stayed at home. Isaac favored Esau for his hunting skills, but Rebekah loved Jacob. One day, Esau came home famished and asked Jacob for some lentil stew. Jacob agreed, but only if Esau sold him his birthright. Esau, thinking he was about to die, agreed and sold his birthright to Jacob.

**Isaac and Abimelech:**
During a famine, Isaac went to Abimelech, king of the Philistines, in Gerar. God told Isaac not to go to Egypt but to stay in the land He would show him. God promised to bless Isaac and his descendants, just as He had promised Abraham. Isaac stayed in Gerar but told the men there that Rebekah was his sister, fearing they would kill him for her beauty. Abimelech discovered the truth and warned his people not to harm Isaac or Rebekah.

Isaac prospered in Gerar, becoming very wealthy, which made the Philistines envious. They filled the wells that Abraham's servants had dug. Abimelech asked Isaac to leave because he had become too powerful. Isaac moved to the valley of Gerar and reopened the wells. He faced disputes over water rights but eventually found peace at a well he named Rehoboth, meaning "room."

God appeared to Isaac in Beer-sheba, reaffirming His promise to bless him. Isaac built an altar and called on God's name. Abimelech came to make a peace treaty with Isaac, recognizing that God was with him. They made a covenant, and Isaac's servants found water, naming the well Shibah, which led to the city's name, Beer-sheba.

**Esau's Marriages:**
Esau married Judith and Basemath, both Hittites, which caused grief to Isaac and Rebekah.

**Isaac's Blessing:**
When Isaac was old and nearly blind, he asked Esau to hunt and prepare a meal so he could bless him. Rebekah overheard and instructed Jacob to bring her two young goats. She prepared a meal for Isaac and dressed Jacob in Esau's clothes, covering his hands and neck with goat skins to mimic Esau's hairiness.Rebekah covered Jacob's hands and neck with goat skins to mimic Esau's hairiness. She then gave Jacob the prepared food and bread to take to his father, Isaac.

Jacob approached Isaac and said, "My father." Isaac responded, "Here I am; who are you, my son?" Jacob replied, "I am Esau, your firstborn. I have done as you asked. Please sit up and eat some of my game so that you may bless me." Isaac asked, "How did you find it so quickly, my son?" Jacob answered, "Because the LORD your God granted me success."

Isaac said to Jacob, "Come closer so I can touch you, my son, to know whether you really are my son Esau or not." Jacob went closer to his father Isaac, who touched him and said, "The voice is the voice of Jacob, but the hands are the hands of Esau." Isaac did not recognize him because his hands were hairy like Esau's, so he blessed him.

Isaac asked again, "Are you really my son Esau?" Jacob replied, "I am." Isaac said, "Bring me some of your game to eat, so that I may give you my blessing." Jacob brought it to him, and Isaac ate. Jacob also brought him wine, and he drank. Then Isaac said, "Come here, my son, and kiss me." Jacob went to him and kissed him. Isaac smelled his clothes and blessed him, saying, "Ah, the smell of my son is like the smell of a field that the LORD has blessed. May God give you heaven's dew and earth's richness—an abundance of grain and new wine. May nations serve you and peoples bow down to you. Be lord over your brothers, and may the sons of your mother bow down to you. May those who curse you be cursed and those who bless you be blessed."

As soon as Isaac finished blessing Jacob and Jacob had scarcely left his father's presence, Esau returned from hunting. He too prepared some tasty food and brought it to his father. He said, "My father, please sit up and eat some of my game, so that you may give me your blessing." Isaac asked, "Who are you?" Esau replied, "I am your son, your firstborn, Esau." Isaac trembled violently and said, "Who was it, then, that hunted game and brought it to me? I ate it just before you came and I blessed him—and indeed he will be blessed!"

When Esau heard his father's words, he burst out with a loud and bitter cry and said to his father, "Bless me—me too, my father!" But Isaac said, "Your brother came deceitfully and took your blessing." Esau said, "Isn't he rightly named Jacob? He has deceived me these two times: He took my birthright, and now he's taken my blessing!" Then he asked, "Haven't you reserved any blessing for me?" Isaac answered Esau, "I have made him lord over you and have made all his relatives his servants, and I have sustained him with grain and new wine. So what can I possibly do for you, my son?" Esau said to his father, "Do you have only one blessing, my father? Bless me too, my father!" Then Esau wept aloud.

His father Isaac answered him, "Your dwelling will be away from the earth's richness, away from the dew of heaven above. You will live by the sword and you will serve your brother. But when you grow restless, you will throw his yoke from off your neck."

Esau held a grudge against Jacob because of the blessing his father had given him. He said to himself, "The days of mourning for my father are near; then I will kill my brother Jacob."

When Rebekah was told what her older son Esau had said, she sent for her younger son Jacob and said to him, "Your brother Esau is planning to avenge himself by killing you. Now then, my son, do what I say: Flee at once to my brother Laban in Haran. Stay with him for a while until your brother's fury subsides. When your brother is no longer angry with you and forgets what you did to him, I'll send word for you to come back from there. Why should I lose both of you in one day?"

Then Rebekah said to Isaac, "I'm disgusted with living because of these Hittite women. If Jacob takes a wife from among the women of this land, from Hittite women like these, my life will not be worth living."

So Isaac called for Jacob and blessed him. Then he commanded him, "Do not marry a Canaanite woman. Go at once to Paddan Aram, to the house of your mother's father Bethuel. Take a wife for yourself there, from among the daughters of Laban, your mother's brother. May God Almighty bless you and make you fruitful and increase your numbers until you become a community of peoples. May he give you and your descendants the blessing given to Abraham, so that you may take possession of the land where you now reside as a foreigner, the land God gave to Abraham." Then Isaac sent Jacob on his way, and he went to Paddan Aram, to Laban son of Bethuel the Aramean, the brother of Rebekah, who was the mother of Jacob and Esau.

Now Esau learned that Isaac had blessed Jacob and had sent him to Paddan Aram to take a wife from there, and that when he blessed him he commanded him, "Do not marry a Canaanite woman," and that Jacob had obeyed his father and mother and had gone to Paddan Aram. Esau then realized how displeasing the Canaanite women were to his father Isaac; so he went to Ishmael and married Mahalath, the sister of Nebaioth and daughter of Ishmael son of Abraham, in addition to the wives he already had.

Jacob left Beersheba and set out for Haran. When he reached a certain place, he stopped for the night because the sun had set. Taking one of the stones there, he put it under his head and lay down to sleep. He had a dream in which he saw a stairway resting on the earth, with its top reaching to heaven, and the angels of God were ascending and descending on it. There above it stood the LORD, and he said, "I am the LORD, the God of your father Abraham and the God of Isaac. I will give you and your descendants the land on which you are lying. Your descendants will be like the dust of the earth, and you will spread out to the west and to the east, to the north and to the south. All peoples on earth will be blessed through you and your offspring. I am with you and will watch over you wherever you go, and I will bring you back to this land. I will not leave you until I have done what I have promised you."

When Jacob awoke from his sleep, he thought, "Surely the LORD is in this place, and I was not aware of it." He was afraid and said, "How awesome is this place! This is none other than the house of God; this is the gate of heaven." Early the next morning Jacob took the stone he had placed under his head and set it up as a pillar and poured oil on top of it. He called that place Bethel, though the city used to be called Luz.

Then Jacob made a vow, saying, "If God will be with me and will watch over me on this journey I am taking and will give me food to eat and clothes to wear so that I return safely to my father's household, then the LORD will be my God and this stone that I have set up as a pillar will be God's house, and of all that you give me I will give you a tenth."

Jacob continued on his journey and came to the land of the eastern peoples. There he saw a well in the open country, with three flocks of sheep lying near it because the flocks were watered from that well. The stone over the mouth of the well was large. When all the flocks were gathered there, the shepherds would roll the stone away from the well's mouth and water the sheep. Then they would return the stone to its place over the mouth of the well.

Jacob asked the shepherds, "My brothers, where are you from?" "We're from Haran," they replied. He said to them, "Do you know Laban, Nahor's grandson?" "Yes, we know him," they answered. Then Jacob asked them, "Is he well?" "Yes, he is," they said, "and here comes his daughter Rachel with the sheep."

"Look," he said, "the sun is still high; it is not time for the flocks to be gathered. Water the sheep and take them back to pasture." "We can't," they replied, "until all the flocks are gathered and the stone has been rolled away from the mouth of the well. Then we will water the sheep."

While he was still talking with them, Rachel came with her father's sheep, for she was a shepherd. When Jacob saw Rachel daughter of his uncle Laban, and Laban's sheep, he went over and rolled the stone away from the mouth of the well and watered his uncle's sheep. Then Jacob kissed Rachel and began to weep aloud. He had told Rachel that he was a relative of her father and a son of Rebekah. So she ran and told her father. As soon as Laban heard the news about Jacob, his sister's son, he hurried to meet him. He embraced him and kissed him and brought him to his home.Jacob stayed with Laban for a month. Laban then said to Jacob, "Just because you're my relative, should you work for me for nothing? Tell me what your wages should be."

Laban had two daughters: Leah, the older one, and Rachel, the younger one. Leah had weak eyes, but Rachel was beautiful. Jacob loved Rachel and said, "I'll work for you for seven years in exchange for your younger daughter, Rachel."

Laban agreed, saying, "It's better for her to marry you than someone else. Stay with me." So Jacob worked seven years for Rachel, but it felt like only a few days to him because he loved her so much.

After the seven years, Jacob said to Laban, "Give me my wife so I can be with her." Laban gathered all the men of the place and held a feast. But in the evening, he took Leah to Jacob, and Jacob slept with her. Laban also gave Leah a servant named Zilpah.

In the morning, Jacob realized it was Leah and confronted Laban, "Why did you deceive me? I worked for Rachel, not Leah!"

Laban replied, "It's not our custom to give the younger daughter before the older one. Finish this bridal week with Leah, and I'll give you Rachel as well, in exchange for another seven years of work."

Jacob agreed, and after the bridal week with Leah, Laban gave him Rachel too. Laban also gave Rachel a servant named Bilhah. Jacob loved Rachel more than Leah and worked for Laban another seven years.

The Lord saw that Leah was unloved, so He enabled her to have children, while Rachel remained childless. Leah had a son named Reuben, saying, "The Lord has seen my misery; now my husband will love me." She had another son named Simeon, saying, "The Lord heard I was unloved and gave me this one too." She had a third son named Levi, saying, "Now my husband will become attached to me because I've given him three sons." She had a fourth son named Judah, saying, "This time I will praise the Lord." Then she stopped having children.

Rachel, seeing she couldn't have children, envied Leah and said to Jacob, "Give me children, or I'll die!" Jacob became angry and said, "Am I in God's place, who has kept you from having children?" Rachel then gave her servant Bilhah to Jacob as a wife, and Bilhah had a son named Dan. Bilhah had another son named Naphtali, and Rachel said, "I've had a great struggle with my sister, and I've won."

Leah, seeing she had stopped having children, gave her servant Zilpah to Jacob as a wife. Zilpah had a son named Gad and another named Asher. Leah said, "How happy I am! The women will call me happy."

During the wheat harvest, Reuben found some mandrakes and brought them to Leah. Rachel asked for some, and Leah replied, "Isn't it enough you took my husband? Now you want my son's mandrakes too?" Rachel agreed to let Leah spend the night with Jacob in exchange for the mandrakes. Leah conceived again and had a fifth son named Issachar, saying, "God has rewarded me for giving my servant to my husband." She had a sixth son named Zebulun, saying, "God has given me a good gift. Now my husband will treat me with honor because I've given him six sons." She also had a daughter named Dinah.

God remembered Rachel and enabled her to conceive. She had a son named Joseph, saying, "May the Lord add another son to me."

After Rachel had Joseph, Jacob asked Laban to let him return to his homeland with his wives and children. Laban, realizing he had been blessed because of Jacob, asked him to name his wages. Jacob proposed to continue tending Laban's flocks in exchange for all the speckled, spotted, and dark-colored animals. Laban agreed, but then separated those animals from the rest of the flock, putting a three-day journey between them and Jacob.

Jacob used a clever breeding technique to ensure that the stronger animals produced speckled and spotted offspring, which became his. As a result, Jacob became very wealthy, with large flocks, servants, camels, and donkeys.

Laban's sons noticed Jacob's growing wealth and accused him of taking what belonged to their father. Laban's attitude toward Jacob also changed. The Lord then told Jacob to return to his homeland, promising to be with him. Jacob called Rachel and Leah to the field and explained the situation, including how Laban had repeatedly changed his wages but God had protected him. Jacob recounted a dream where God showed him that all the male goats mating with the flock were streaked, speckled, or spotted, confirming that God had seen Laban's unfair treatment.Jacob said, "I am the God of Bethel, where you anointed a pillar and made a vow to Me. Now, get up, leave this land, and return to your homeland."

Rachel and Leah replied, "Do we have any share or inheritance left in our father's house? He treats us like strangers, selling us and consuming our money. All the wealth God took from our father belongs to us and our children. So, do whatever God has told you."

Jacob then got up, put his children and wives on camels, and took all his livestock and possessions he had acquired in Paddan-aram to go to his father Isaac in the land of Canaan.

While Laban was away shearing his sheep, Rachel stole her father's household gods. Jacob deceived Laban the Aramean by not telling him he was fleeing. He left with everything he had, crossed the river, and headed for the hill country of Gilead.

Three days later, Laban was informed that Jacob had fled. He took his relatives with him and pursued Jacob for seven days, catching up with him in the hill country of Gilead. But God came to Laban in a dream and warned him not to say anything good or bad to Jacob.

When Laban reached Jacob, he said, "What have you done? You deceived me and took my daughters like captives of war. Why did you run away secretly and deceive me? You didn't even let me kiss my grandchildren and daughters goodbye. You acted foolishly. I have the power to harm you, but the God of your father spoke to me last night and warned me not to say anything to you. Now, you have gone because you longed to return to your father's house, but why did you steal my gods?"

Jacob answered, "I was afraid you would take your daughters from me by force. If you find anyone with your gods, they shall not live. In the presence of our relatives, see for yourself what is yours and take it." Jacob did not know that Rachel had stolen the gods.

Laban searched Jacob's tent, Leah's tent, and the tents of the two maidservants but found nothing. When he entered Rachel's tent, she had hidden the gods in the camel's saddle and was sitting on them. She said to her father, "Don't be angry, my lord, that I cannot stand up in your presence; I'm having my period." So, Laban searched but did not find the gods.

Jacob became angry and confronted Laban, "What is my crime? What sin have I committed that you pursued me? You have searched through all my belongings and found nothing of yours. Let my relatives and yours decide between us. I have been with you for twenty years. Your sheep and goats have not miscarried, and I have not eaten rams from your flocks. I did not bring you animals torn by wild beasts; I bore the loss myself. You demanded payment from me for anything stolen by day or night. I endured scorching heat by day and cold by night, and sleep fled from my eyes. I have worked for you for twenty years—fourteen years for your two daughters and six years for your flocks, and you changed my wages ten times. If the God of my father, the God of Abraham, and the Fear of Isaac had not been with me, you would have sent me away empty-handed. But God has seen my hardship and the toil of my hands, and last night He rebuked you."

Laban answered, "The women are my daughters, the children are my children, and the flocks are my flocks. Everything you see is mine. Yet, what can I do today about these daughters of mine or the children they have borne? Come now, let's make a covenant, you and I, and let it serve as a witness between us."

Jacob took a stone and set it up as a pillar. He said to his relatives, "Gather some stones." They took stones and piled them in a heap, and they ate there by the heap. Laban called it Jegar-sahadutha, and Jacob called it Galeed. Laban said, "This heap is a witness between you and me today." That is why it was called Galeed. It was also called Mizpah because he said, "May the Lord keep watch between you and me when we are away from each other. If you mistreat my daughters or take other wives besides my daughters, even though no one is with us, remember that God is a witness between you and me."

Laban also said to Jacob, "Here is this heap, and here is this pillar I have set up between you and me. This heap is a witness, and this pillar is a witness, that I will not go past this heap to your side to harm you, and you will not go past this heap and pillar to my side to harm me. May the God of Abraham and the God of Nahor, the God of their father, judge between us." So Jacob took an oath in the name of the Fear of his father Isaac. He offered a sacrifice there in the hill country and invited his relatives to a meal. After they had eaten, they spent the night there.

Early the next morning, Laban kissed his grandchildren and daughters and blessed them. Then he left and returned home.

Jacob continued on his way, and the angels of God met him. When Jacob saw them, he said, "This is the camp of God!" So he named that place Mahanaim.

Jacob sent messengers ahead of him to his brother Esau in the land of Seir, the country of Edom. He instructed them, "This is what you are to say to my lord Esau: 'Your servant Jacob says, I have been staying with Laban and have remained there till now. I have cattle and donkeys, sheep and goats, male and female servants. Now I am sending this message to my lord, that I may find favor in your eyes.'"

When the messengers returned to Jacob, they said, "We went to your brother Esau, and now he is coming to meet you with four hundred men."

Jacob was greatly afraid and distressed. He divided the people who were with him into two groups, along with the flocks, herds, and camels. He thought, "If Esau comes and attacks one group, the other group may escape."

Jacob prayed, "O God of my father Abraham, God of my father Isaac, Lord, you who said to me, 'Go back to your country and your relatives, and I will make you prosper,' I am unworthy of all the kindness and faithfulness you have shown your servant. I had only my staff when I crossed this Jordan, but now I have become two camps. Save me, I pray, from the hand of my brother Esau, for I am afraid he will come and attack me, and also the mothers with their children. But you have said, 'I will surely make you prosper and will make your descendants like the sand of the sea, which cannot be counted.'"

Jacob spent the night there, and from what he had with him, he selected a gift for his brother Esau: two hundred female goats and twenty male goats, two hundred ewes and twenty rams, thirty female camels with their young, forty cows and ten bulls, and twenty female donkeys and ten male donkeys. He put them in the care of his servants, each herd by itself, and said to his servants, "Go ahead of me, and keep some space between the herds."

He instructed the one in the lead, "When my brother Esau meets you and asks, 'Who do you belong to, and where are you going, and who owns all these animals in front of you?' then you are to say, 'They belong to your servant Jacob. They are a gift sent to my lord Esau, and he is coming behind us.'"

He also instructed the second, the third, and all the others who followed the herds: "You are to say the same thing to Esau when you meet him. And be sure to say, 'Your servant Jacob is coming behind us.'" For he thought, "I will pacify him with these gifts I am sending on ahead; later, when I see him, perhaps he will receive me."

So Jacob's gifts went on ahead of him, but he himself spent the night in the camp.

That night Jacob got up and took his two wives, his two female servants, and his eleven sons and crossed the ford of the Jabbok. After he had sent them across the stream, he sent over all his possessions. So Jacob was left alone, and a man wrestled with him till daybreak. When the man saw that he could not overpower him, he touched the socket of Jacob's hip so that his hip was wrenched as he wrestled with the man. Then the man said, "Let me go, for it is daybreak."

But Jacob replied, "I will not let you go unless you bless me."

The man asked him, "What is your name?"

"Jacob," he answered.

Then the man said, "Your name will no longer be Jacob, but Israel, because you have struggled with God and with humans and have overcome."Jacob's name was changed to Israel because he had struggled with God and humans and had prevailed. Jacob then asked the man for his name, but the man did not answer and instead blessed him. Jacob named the place Peniel, saying, "I have seen God face to face, and yet my life was spared." As the sun rose, Jacob limped because of his hip. This is why the Israelites do not eat the tendon attached to the hip socket, because Jacob's hip was touched there.

When Jacob saw Esau approaching with 400 men, he divided his children among Leah, Rachel, and the two maidservants. He placed the maidservants and their children in front, Leah and her children next, and Rachel and Joseph last. Jacob himself went ahead and bowed to the ground seven times as he approached Esau. Esau ran to meet Jacob, embraced him, kissed him, and they both wept. Esau then saw the women and children and asked who they were. Jacob replied, "These are the children God has graciously given your servant."

The maidservants and their children came forward and bowed down, followed by Leah and her children, and finally Joseph and Rachel. Esau asked about the meaning of all the animals he had met, and Jacob explained they were a gift to find favor in Esau's eyes. Esau said he had enough and told Jacob to keep what he had, but Jacob insisted, saying seeing Esau's face was like seeing the face of God, and Esau had received him favorably. Jacob urged Esau to accept the gift, and Esau did.

Esau suggested they travel together, but Jacob explained that the children and young animals needed to move at a slower pace. Esau offered to leave some of his men with Jacob, but Jacob declined, wanting only to find favor in Esau's eyes. Esau then returned to Seir, and Jacob went to Succoth, where he built a house and shelters for his livestock.

Jacob later arrived safely in Shechem in Canaan and bought a piece of land from the sons of Hamor for 100 pieces of silver. He set up an altar there and called it El-Elohe-Israel.

Dinah, Jacob's daughter, went out to visit the women of the land. Shechem, the son of Hamor, saw her, took her, and violated her. He then fell in love with her and spoke tenderly to her. Shechem asked his father to get Dinah as his wife. Jacob heard about the incident but waited for his sons to return from the fields before taking action. Hamor went to speak with Jacob, and Jacob's sons were furious when they heard what had happened. Hamor asked for Dinah to be given to Shechem as a wife and proposed intermarriage between their peoples. Shechem offered to pay any bride price for Dinah.

Jacob's sons deceitfully agreed to the marriage on the condition that all the males in Shechem be circumcised. Hamor and Shechem agreed and convinced the men of their city to be circumcised. Three days later, while the men were still in pain, Simeon and Levi, Dinah's brothers, attacked the city, killing all the males, including Hamor and Shechem. They rescued Dinah and plundered the city, taking livestock, women, and children.

Jacob rebuked Simeon and Levi, fearing retaliation from the Canaanites and Perizzites. They defended their actions, saying their sister should not be treated like a prostitute.

God told Jacob to go to Bethel and build an altar. Jacob instructed his household to get rid of foreign gods, purify themselves, and change their clothes. They gave Jacob all their foreign gods and earrings, which he buried under an oak tree near Shechem. As they traveled, God instilled fear in the surrounding cities, so no one pursued them. Jacob arrived in Bethel, built an altar, and called the place El-Bethel. Deborah, Rebekah's nurse, died and was buried under an oak below Bethel.

God appeared to Jacob again, blessed him, and reaffirmed his name change to Israel. God promised that nations and kings would come from him and that the land given to Abraham and Isaac would be given to him and his descendants. Jacob set up a stone pillar at the place where God had spoken to him, poured a drink offering on it, and called the place Bethel.Jacob named the place where God spoke to him Bethel. 

As they traveled from Bethel, Rachel went into labor and had a difficult childbirth. The midwife reassured her, saying, "Don't be afraid; you have another son." As Rachel was dying, she named her son Ben-Oni, but Jacob called him Benjamin. Rachel died and was buried on the way to Ephrath, which is Bethlehem. Jacob set up a pillar on her grave, which still stands today.

Jacob had twelve sons: 
- Leah's sons: Reuben (Jacob's firstborn), Simeon, Levi, Judah, Issachar, and Zebulun.
- Rachel's sons: Joseph and Benjamin.
- Bilhah's sons (Rachel's maidservant): Dan and Naphtali.
- Zilpah's sons (Leah's maidservant): Gad and Asher.

Jacob, now called Israel, moved and set up camp beyond Migdal Eder. While living there, Reuben slept with Bilhah, his father's concubine, and Israel found out.

Jacob then went to visit his father Isaac in Mamre, near Hebron, where Abraham and Isaac had lived. Isaac lived to be 180 years old, then he died and was buried by his sons Esau and Jacob.

Esau's family history is as follows:
- Esau, also known as Edom, took wives from the Canaanites: Adah (daughter of Elon the Hittite), Oholibamah (daughter of Anah, granddaughter of Zibeon the Hivite), and Basemath (Ishmael's daughter, sister of Nebaioth).
- Adah bore Eliphaz, Basemath bore Reuel, and Oholibamah bore Jeush, Jalam, and Korah.

Esau moved his family and possessions to a land away from Jacob because their wealth was too great for them to live together. Esau settled in the hill country of Seir, also known as Edom.

Joseph, at seventeen, was tending the flocks with his brothers, the sons of Bilhah and Zilpah. He brought a bad report about them to their father. Israel loved Joseph more than his other sons because he was born in his old age, and he made Joseph a richly ornamented robe. This favoritism caused Joseph's brothers to hate him.

Joseph had a dream where his sheaf of grain stood upright while his brothers' sheaves gathered around and bowed down to it. His brothers hated him even more for his dreams and words. He had another dream where the sun, moon, and eleven stars bowed down to him. When he told this to his father and brothers, his father rebuked him, asking if they would indeed bow down to him. His brothers were jealous, but his father kept the matter in mind.

Esau's descendants included chiefs and kings who ruled in Edom before any king reigned over the Israelites. Some of these kings were Bela, Jobab, Husham, Hadad, Samlah, Shaul, Baal-Hanan, and Hadar.

Joseph's brothers went to graze their father's flocks near Shechem. Israel sent Joseph to check on them. When Joseph found them in Dothan, his brothers saw him coming and plotted to kill him, calling him "the dreamer." They planned to throw him into a pit and say a wild animal devoured him. Reuben intervened, suggesting they not kill him but throw him into a pit, intending to rescue him later. When Joseph arrived, they stripped him of his ornate robe and threw him into an empty pit. As they sat to eat, they saw a caravan of Ishmaelites heading to Egypt. Judah suggested selling Joseph to the Ishmaelites instead of killing him."Let's not harm him ourselves. Instead, let's sell him to the Ishmaelites since he is our brother, our own flesh and blood." His brothers agreed. 

Meanwhile, a group of Midianite merchants passed by. They pulled Joseph out of the pit and sold him to the Ishmaelites for twenty pieces of silver. The Ishmaelites then took Joseph to Egypt. 

When Reuben returned to the pit and saw that Joseph was gone, he tore his clothes in distress. He went back to his brothers and said, "The boy is gone! What am I going to do now?" 

They took Joseph's colorful coat, killed a goat, and dipped the coat in its blood. They brought the coat to their father and said, "We found this. See if it's your son's coat." Jacob recognized it and said, "It is my son's coat! A wild animal must have devoured him. Joseph has surely been torn to pieces." Jacob tore his clothes, put on sackcloth, and mourned for his son for many days. All his sons and daughters tried to comfort him, but he refused to be comforted, saying, "I will go to my grave mourning for my son." And Jacob wept for him.

Meanwhile, the Midianites sold Joseph in Egypt to Potiphar, one of Pharaoh's officials, the captain of the guard.

At that time, Judah left his brothers and went to stay with a man named Hirah in Adullam. There, Judah met the daughter of a Canaanite named Shua. He married her, and she bore him three sons: Er, Onan, and Shelah. Judah found a wife named Tamar for his firstborn, Er. But Er was wicked in the Lord's sight, so the Lord put him to death. Judah then told Onan to fulfill his duty to his brother by marrying Tamar and producing offspring for him. However, Onan knew the offspring would not be his, so he spilled his semen on the ground to avoid providing offspring for his brother. This act displeased the Lord, so He put Onan to death as well. Judah then told Tamar to live as a widow in her father's house until his son Shelah grew up, fearing that Shelah might also die like his brothers. So Tamar went to live in her father's house.

Joseph was taken to Egypt and sold to Potiphar, an officer of Pharaoh and captain of the guard. The Lord was with Joseph, and he prospered in Potiphar's house. Potiphar saw that the Lord was with Joseph and made him his personal attendant, putting him in charge of his household and everything he owned. From the time Joseph was put in charge, the Lord blessed Potiphar's household because of Joseph. Potiphar left everything he had in Joseph's care, only concerning himself with the food he ate. Joseph was well-built and handsome.

After some time, Judah's wife, the daughter of Shua, died. After mourning, Judah went to Timnah to shear his sheep, accompanied by his friend Hirah. Tamar was told that her father-in-law was going to Timnah, so she took off her widow's clothes, covered herself with a veil, and sat at the entrance to Enaim, on the way to Timnah. She did this because she saw that Shelah had grown up, but she had not been given to him as a wife. When Judah saw her, he thought she was a prostitute because she had covered her face. He approached her and asked to sleep with her, not realizing she was his daughter-in-law. She asked what he would give her, and he promised a young goat from his flock. She asked for a pledge until he sent it, and he gave her his seal, cord, and staff. They slept together, and she became pregnant. She then left and put on her widow's clothes again.

Judah sent the young goat with his friend Hirah to retrieve his pledge, but Hirah couldn't find her. He asked the men of the place about the prostitute, but they said there had been no prostitute there. Hirah returned to Judah and told him he couldn't find her, and the men said there was no prostitute. Judah said, "Let her keep what she has, or we will become a laughingstock. I sent her this goat, but you couldn't find her."
